RATING: 8
I wouldn't have believed it if I hadn't tried it -- a fun, dice-based [i]civ[/i] game.  Plays pretty quickly, though there will be some downtime between turns.  Relatively little player interaction (limited availability of monuments, some disasters affect opponents).  But darnit, it (sorta) feels like a civ game!

The dice-rolling doesn't impinge on the underlying mechanics.  Initially, you want everything -- it becomes a matter of prioritizing your goals followed by luck of the rolls.  Later on, as the game progresses and you develop a strategy, your preferences become more concrete and directed.  The inclusion of a food requirement serves to temper your goals.  I like the implementation of the disasters since they have a very real risk/reward balance.

Oddly enough, I like this game.  It will probably go into my "longer filler" category (playtime at 20-40 min.), particularly as there is a medium-strength luck element.

Also, I have a sneaky suspicion this may become another pseudo/almost-gateway game for 2-4 players.  Easy to explain, easy to play, but with a small side of strategy and options.  I don't think it would be overwhelming, particularly as a follow up gateway game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
FAMILY, STRATEGY

This game immediately reminds one of Yahtzee and Stone Age, two completely different games united by their use of dice. Roll dice to earn money, acquire resources, recruit workers, and build cities, monuments and developments. Lots of strategic choices and even interactivity, as you race against others to build cities first for more points and avoid the consequences of others' "disasters."

Probably would be a good game to take along camping! The components are wonderful: large dice with unique symbols and thick wood game boards that remind me of cribbage.

Recommended with 1-4 players (best 2). 30-45 mins.

You roll dice to match symbols in order to fill out a pad to build structures. build armies, do agriculture, etc. But it doesn't feel like you are doing anything but rolling dice. \_______GER_______/
Eine Priese Würfel-Kniffle und eine Portion Aufbau in einer durchaus spannenden Kombination zusammen gebracht.
Das Spielmaterial aus Holz ist sehr ansprechend.
Insgesamt ein kurzweiliges Spiel, bei dem man doch etwas darüber grübeln kann, wie man seine Würfel und Rohstoffe am Besten einsetzen soll. Und dabei gibt es doch schon eine Menge Alternativen zu bedenken (wenn die Würfel nicht vollkommen gegen einen sind). 
Ich habe das Spiel jetzt auch in der Solo-variante schätzen gelernt und dadurch meine Beurteilung weiter nach oben verschoben. Beim Solospiel hat man nur 10 Züge Zeit seine Siegpunkte zu optimieren. Eine sehr gute Beschreibung von dem Spiel ist hier zu finden: [geekurl=/thread/380167/a-comprehensive-pictorial-overview-pandemics-desi]Bronze Age Solo[/geekurl]

[thing=47389]Späte Bronzezeit[/thing] ist eine sehr gute Erweiterung wenn man die (einfacheren) Grundregeln von der Bronzezeit schon durchgespielt hat. Auch als Solo weiterhin gut spielbar (gleiche Regeln wie im Standard Spiel), und natürlich machen im Solospiel auch die Schiffe zum Handel (Austausch von Waren) Sinn.

RATING: 7
What a wonderfully little dice game this is. The game comes with some really nice wooden dice and goods boards, along with a ton of pages and a short rules sheat.

It has the typical Yahtzee-style double re-roll mechanics de rigueur for these games, but it imports some features from its bigger games to give this filler some weight. For example, RttA adds on some slight engine building elements with the construction of new 'cities' (=dice) and developments.

Definitely worth seeking out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A very clever game that just isn't that interesting.  It's of a similar vein to Puerto Rico and its ilk in that it has a limited lifespan built in where you are discovering how the special powers and game systems work and after that initial period it's not quite as interesting.  RTtA, unfortunately, does not even manage a respectable number of plays before the diminishing returns kick in.  I would say 5-10 plays and the various building interactions should be fully elucidated.

Of course, even understanding the strategies of the game does nothing to help you with the dice.  It's pretty clear that building a few cities is crucial at the beginning to get more dice.  If you don't roll citizens, you aren't getting a city and you are going to lose to someone that does.  It's that simple.  Variations on this theme crop up throughout the game and it really undermines the great feel of a deep, strategic civilization game that it has going for it.

My last complaint is the solitary nature of the game.  Some propose using trading rules to alleviate this, but that requires more players (I don't see much reason to trade with 2 players) which means the game is nearly proportionately longer.  More importantly, it's not clear to me that it is worth the effort to engage in furious trading for such a fluffy game.

Still, I am impressed with what was done here.  All the elements of a civilization game in such a short and minimal package.  I think it keenly lays bare just how much unnecessary chrome is welded onto the many popular civilization games here (I am of course looking at Through the Ages).

RATING: 5
Pretty much what you would expect from a dice game. Roll dice, put some to one side, roll again, put more to one side, roll a third time.

Interaction is very limited - but way its implemented annoys me, seems turn order gives an advantage to early players. You take your turn, roll dice, get stuff then turn moves on - but game ends as soon as one player hits the trigger, no attempt to give everyone an equal number of turns, so sucks to go later assuming 'equal' luck ...

Also monuments - first to finish one gets the main points, so again, better to be doing it before someone else. Oh and they didn't name them ... would have been better if they had - and had them as central tiles you took or something.

Colours are bad for those who are colourblind - I couldn't differentiate between the 2 'browns' nor the 2 'greens' :( 

All in all, 'ok' quite like the ideas behind it - just was hoping for a little more.

As a filler it works, but there's a bit of counting/consideration for spending your coins/wares which can slow the game down.

I'd play it, just in no rush to own it. Especially at the price, for a fiver, yes ... over 3x that, no thanks.

RATING: 8
I really enjoy this game. I was introduced to it by a good friend (Bobby4th) back in Arizona, and purchased it as soon as my local game shop had it in stock...which took much longer than I would have liked.

Despite its resemblance (graphically and in name) to Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization, it has a very different feel.  Roll Through the Ages is obviously a much lighter game, and is certainly much quicker to play.  It has a bit of a press-your-luck feel without the frustration that something like Yahtzee might have.  (Incidentally, did Yahtzee pioneer the dice-freezing action, or is it simply the poster child?)

What I like about RTtA is that I have multiple paths to victory; I can focus on building monuments quickly, or I can focus on developments, or I can focus on a combination of the two.  In some ways, the dice will drive your strategy, but for the most part, you can decide on your own course of action.

The only negative (and it's not a huge one) is that there isn't a lot of player interaction, at least not out of the box.  In essence, the only things that can happen are that another player's disaster affects you, or another player builds a monument first (thus denying you points).  Not a huge problem, but don't go into this game expecting a ton of interaction.

Overall a solid game, and definitely one I'm pleased to have in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
To reiterate: I only play this game with trading :) I believe that the trading is central to the design here, and to play without it makes the game a little too frustratingly random and solitaire-y for my tastes; trading provides an important outlet to help players roll with the luck of the dice, helps to keep it at the 45 minute mark (or less) by making the game in general richer, and is an important factor in the balance between commodities and monuments. Originally, trading was not an optional rule; it was made optional at the publisher's request. I lobbied Matt pretty hard to keep it in the core rules, but I ended up on the losing end of that one. With trading, this is a nice, light-to-medium game with fun, flavor, and some strategy which I like quite a bit. Without it ... it's OK but too solitaire-y for my tastes at best and irritatingly overlong at worst.
---
I finally got to play the shipping version of the game (a bunch of my "Unpublished Prototype" plays are this game). As someone who was involved quite a bit in the playtesting of this game, I'm pretty happy with how it came out. There are a lot of moving parts here, and I was a little nervous the balance wasn't going to be quite right - getting the monument/commodity/cities balance right was tricky. But so far, I think it is. (I'm not sure I ever played the final final version before it was published, the game was moving around a lot during the development process.)

I think 3 is probably the sweet spot here in terms of number of players. That way, you can get some trading action, but there isn't too much downtime (personally, I would never play the no-trading version). There can be some AP, so watch for that. My feeling is that at 30-45 minutes it's a fun game, but some players will drag it out past that.
---
For the folks who have been following this game, I will offer up one little insider tidbit. It actually does have a relationship to Through the Ages, although not the one you might expect. As Matt mentions in the BoardGameNews interview, this game came out of a one-month Civilization Dice Game design challenge our little gaming group had (our games were crap, but Matt's was good :)). The idea of doing a light, fast, civilization-building dice game was actually a direct result of my extreme frustration with being dragged through another excruciating 6-hour game of Through the Ages, a game I decided I never, ever wanted to see again.

RATING: N/A
played
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Updated 11/7/10. 

[thing=37380]Roll Through the Ages[/thing] was another new-to-me game that I had high hopes for -- hopes that were ultimately dashed.  I had heard from several users that the game provided a reasonably short, lightweight, interesting dice-based experience.  Although I'm not the world's largest dice fan, I do enjoy having multiple tense, tactical [geeklist=23110]super-fillers[/geeklist] in my collection for weeknights with my wife: [thread=577318]Seeland[/thread], [thing=38054]Snow Tails[/thing], [thing=8051]Attika[/thing], [thing=35935]Day & Night[/thing], [thing=34297]The Climbers[/thing], and [thing=27173]Vikings[/thing] all come to mind.

The implementation of Roll Through the Ages fails on multiple levels, however.  First, there is a defined early strategy: roll workers.  Yes, I realize that's roughly equivalent to saying that the key to [thing=3522]LCR[/thing] is to [thread=433105]roll well and not roll poorly[/thread].  But players who don't roll workers early can't get extra dice, and there is virtually no downside to having extra dice.  In fact, it seems that the game's central struggle is between the players who don't roll workers early attempting to end the game quickly (which never seemed particularly viable) and the players who do roll workers early hoping to have enough turns to exploit their dice advantage (which seems much more common).  So: roll well and don't roll poorly.

[b]Random Off-Topic Rant:[/b]
[o]As a quick aside, I often complain about Eurogames being too balanced, so my complaint about the imbalance in the importance of various resources early in the game might initially appear hypocritical.  The difference is that Roll Through the Ages uses dice to deliver imbalanced goods to players randomly.  Most well-crafted dice games these days are about providing players opportunities to manage that randomness (Stone Age) or providing players multiple viable paths to work within the randomly imposed constraints (Alien Frontiers).  At least initially, Roll Through the Ages appears to do neither.  Instead, it dictates a single, viable early game path that depends on players obtaining a sufficient quantity of a randomly distributed resource.  This seems like poor design, and is substantially different from games that provide imbalanced opportunities for players to choose (or not) to compete over.[/o]

Perhaps in an attempt to mitigate some of the randomness I just complained of, Roll Through the Ages allows players up to three re-rolls, setting aside any number of dice you wish to keep in the process.  Yes, this is Civ-[thing=2243]Yahtzee[/thing].  The downside to this attempt at balance is that the game is so transparently calculable that anyone actively competing for a win has little choice but to succumb to AP.  This should be the gameplay description on the back of the box, just to warn people:

[q]Roll some dice. If you don't like them, re-roll as many as you want (except skulls). If you don't like those, re-roll as many as you want (except skulls). 

Between each of these rolls, calculate the best possible allocation of dice (using scratch paper if necessary). Then calculate the odds of increasing that tally by re-rolling any single die. Then calculate the odds of increasing that tally by re-rolling any combination of dice. The ask whether this is your first, second, or third roll. Then remember that you upgraded one skill so that you get extra resources. Then recalculate to account for this skill. Then ask whether this is your first, second, or third roll.  

Are you having fun yet?[/q]
People talk about some games feeling like a spreadsheet, but Roll Through the Ages takes that to a new level.  The thin veneer of theme does little to distract from the constant stream of boring little calculations necessary to decide how to allocate dice and which strategies to purse.  It's a mind-numbing endeavor when it's your turn, and when waiting for others in a 4-player game, you might as well just stand up, stretch your legs, grab a smoke, and maybe make a frittata.  It's going to be a while. 

Third and finally, despite all of the calculation required for smart gameplay, the (apparently theme-required) disaster mechanism is nothing more than a risk/reward push-your-luck game.  Sometimes disasters hurt you, sometimes they hurt others.  They (mostly) can't be re-rolled, so the player who is unlucky enough to roll a ton of them at an inopportune moment will lose the game through no fault of his/her own.  It's an unfortunate mechanic that feels tacked-on to the game because someone in play-testing probably argued vociferously that you can't have a civilization-themed game without random, soul-crushing disasters.  Designers should stop listening to that guy.  

All-in-all, the game feels like a low-budget, poorly-themed, solitaire version of a civ-game.  Unless you can't live without dice, there is nothing that Roll Through the Ages offers that [thing=42910]Peloponnes[/thing] doesn't do exponentially better in a similar time frame (including solo play!).

All-in-all an mildly unpleasant experience with little redeeming value.

RATING: 7

One of my favorite dice games overall.

RATING: 4
I'm not really a dice game type guy, so take these comments for what they're worth with that in mind. RTtA is basically a dice snowball game. You start out with 3 dice, you roll the dice, and those rolls give you the ability to buy more dice which are then used to accrue victory points at the end of the game. Side issues include needing to keep rolling enough food to feed your dice (or face negative VPs) and the ability to invest in special powers that make your rolls better or generally make it easier to buy stuff. Another point of note is the skull sides of the dice which provide significant amounts of money, but which can be bad in quantity if you haven't bought preventative improvements. These can hand out negative points to you, though with appropriate upgrades, you can actually want some of them to come up as they'll affect your opponents instead.

RTtA is the perfect example to me of a game that does nothing particularly wrong, nor anything particularly right. It's nicely produced, and has some flashes of interest, but it isn't particularly compelling either. Workmanlike, but lacking a clever hook that would make me want to come back to it. It's the kind of game that I would play if others were eager, but somewhat reluctantly because of the surfeit of other, higher quality filler that's available. At 30 minutes, it's about 15 minutes too long for what it offers.

Aside: some people really like the added complexity of the Late Bronze Age scoring sheets, personally, I try to avoid them, as they make the game another 10-15 minutes longer. The base game with the LBA sheets rates somewhere around a 3.

RATING: 5
When this game was announced, I didn't even bother to look at it during Spiel. Civilization meets Yathzee, that's the gist of it. While both games scratch a certain itch at times, I don't think the marriage of both ideas is a particularly fruitful one. Players throw a number of dice Yathzee-style, then do things with the values which turn up. Food is necessary to feed cities (which are 1:1 related to the number of dice you are allowed to throw); vases give you goods which allow you to purchase discoveries (and VP); little guys give you new cities or monuments (and VP); coins give you, well, coins for discoveries; skulls are disasters and usually cause point loss. It's all fairly simple and straightforward, and from what I've read, that's what Matt Leacock intended.

I have two problems with this game. Well, three to be honest, but number three is not an issue in practice. The first problem is that I dislike the way the game was produced. The cribbage boards probably were meant to add sturdiness to the proceedings so that the game became portable, but people with big hands will find them fiddly and small. In addition, the game is so multiplayer solitaire that you don't need to see what other people are doing, so why a simpler and lighter  solution was not devised (magnets!) is beyond me. Second issue I have that in the basic setup it can be over before you know it. The game [i]appears[/i] to be a multiple-ways-to-victory thingy, but in practice the goals are reached so quickly that this doesn't come to pass. I understand that Matt has released a longer version with altered scoring sheet for those who prefer their games to last a little longer. But still, it's unsatisfactory. There's quite a bit of stuff to keep track of, and it's over before you know it. Top-heavy ruleset, choices mean little, you name it, it all boils down to the same issue with this design.

RttA is not a bad game, but neither do I find it very good or engaging. It is simple enough to play with my mother, but I doubt she'd able to grasp the details, and that means it will probably fail as a family game as well. The rating hovers between 5 and 6: if my mother understands it it'll become a 6, otherwise the rating will stay put at 5.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The structure and mechanics of this game are really very clever.  I like the risk-taking of the skulls.  I like the varied step ladder for acquiring goods.  And the use of pegs and a board is special.

But my overall opinion for this game goes in 2 directions.  When it comes to the solo game (which I have on my iPhone), I'm fiercely addicted.  But when playing with others, it feels like the game is always over before it's barely started.  The winner is often the player who can force the endgame first, catching the other players off-guard.

That being said, I still want to keep playing this game and learn to get better at it!

WARNING: This game will be loathsome to the luck-averse.

RATING: 7
I rate this game for what it is, and not for what it does not try to be.  It is a lightweight game meant to capture some of the flavor and decision-making found in other civ games that take much longer, with many more components and a larger footprint.  And as that lightweight game, it is an unqualified success.

I would have preferred that the dice be made of the resin/polymer/plastic-whatever material that you find in higher-quality dice, with the icons being in greater contrast to the die color, and being a better fit for the hand of someone not in the NBA, but that is pretty much my only reservation about the game.

I would like to see some expansion dice, with other options.  I think this game system is ripe with potential for such things.

RATING: 6.5
Brilliance lies in its accessability.  Fills the void of a light civilization game that can be learned by anyone and played within probably a half hour.  Uses dice, but Yahtzee-style rerolls allow for better manipulation of results.

One issue I find is that it's always a good play to grow your civilization ASAP (i.e. increase your number of cities, and consequently, the number of dice you get to roll). Because of this, all players are doing the same thing at the beginning, and it comes down to who rolled more workers (workers are needed to expand cities). It would be nice to see a viable alternative to this standard opening.

I'd almost always rather play Ra: The Dice Game over this.

[2010-09-22 Rating: n.a.->6]

This rating is mostly based on my plays of this game on Yucata.de, but I've also played the german version released by Pegasus.

SUMMARY
--------

This is a light civilization game using dice as the main mechanic. Each round you throw as much dice as you possess cities (three in the beginning). After max. three tosses of your dice, the dice determine what you will have available in this round:

- Workers: These allow you to claim more cities or built monuments. The later ones are mostly built as a source of victory points (VPs), more cities grant you more dice.

- Food: Each round you need to have as much food as you have cities. If you have less, you get -1 VP per cities you can't feed.

- Coins: Each coin symbol you see on your dice is worth 7 coins. You can use these coins to built a technology that allows you to break the rules or to give you a scoring bonus. Example: There is a technology that let's you re-roll one die at the end of your turn, another technology gives you one VP per city you own.
Each coin you don't use at the end of your round is lost.

- Wares: Each ware-symbol on a die give you one ware. You can stack them for a maximum of six wares and trade them in for coins later. These can be used to buy technologies.

- Skull: Skulls can be bad and good at the same time. For one, each skull you roll gives you also two wares. This is great, but you can't re-roll any die showing a skull. After you have thrown all your dice for the third time or you passed, you count the number of skulls you rolled. If this number is higher than one, someone, well mostly you, get's punished. The skull mechanism is also the only source of interaction within this game. Roll three skulls and your opponents lose three VPs. Roll two, four or five and you lose 2 or 4 VPs or all your wares.

The game ends after one player has bought his fifth technology or each of the available monuments for that player count has been built by a least one player. You count your VPs and the player with the most of them wins.

COMMENT:
---------

* This is a light beer&pretzels game. There are some valid routes to victory and there is some fun in discovering them. The game plays very quick (~10-15 minutes), but the interaction is quite low. All in all, this is a nice Yahtzee variant with some "push your luck" feel to it. I would play it, if nothing better is around, but I would not suggest it.

RATING: 7
I just found my new favorite Travel Game to replace Pass the Pigs!  More than just a press-your-luck dice game, it incorporates strategy as well.  A diced Euro - what a concept!  A couple minor issues with the components - I received a 1st Ed. copy, and the dice are a tad on the light side, and can be difficult to read.  Also, several of the scoring pegs are very similar in color, which makes them hard to tell apart, especially in poor light.  But the overall quality of the components, and the nice tactile feel of the heavy wood dice make up for that.  This is a great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I'm not a big fan of this style of dice game.  It's fun as a solo endeavor for a while, but it takes too long between turns when you're playing with 4 people (just like To Court the King).  The problem is that there is not enough interaction for people to pay attention in between turns.  It's the same problem as Yahtzee.  Knizia tried to mitigate this problem with Pikamino by introducing some player interaction.  Alan Moon made it so that all players played at the same time with Diamant (which I think is the best solution).  Roll Through the Ages has a little bit of interaction, but there is still no reason to pay attention to the game when it is not your turn.  So people start goofing off and talking and forget to notice when it is their turn.

I also feel like the majority of my score rest upon whether I roll people early in the game.  Any game where I get three dice with people on them for the first turn will be a good one.  This seems like a design flaw.  With Yahtzee, every turn is equal to every other turn, so it doesn't matter if you get unlucky early or unlucky late.  Getting unlucky early in RTtA makes it so you don't want to finish the game. 

Anyhow, I think RTtA is ok as a solo game, but there are other games I'd rather play with any other number of players.

RATING: 6
Players roll dice and check off goals on their reference card, simulating the development of a civilization.  The title of the game is a pun on the fabulous Through The Ages, but the similarities end there.  This game is a Yahtzee spin-off, where players get three rolls, and record their results on their peg board (recording food and goods), and then spend that stuff on maintaining their "cities" (i.e. dice) and purchasing extra cities, monuments and advances.  Nicely produced and a good compact size, but leaving you with a "that's it?" feeling after a couple of games.  Very expensive for what you get (i.e. cheap wooden offcuts ... dice, board, pegs).

RATING: 6
Tricky game to discuss - it looks fairly boring from the outset. A player takes the dice and rolls them several times, and ticks a couple boxes while everyone one else waits for their turn to do the same. And in many ways, it is that boring.

But there is a certain fun to the tactical decisions involved. Do you go for extra dice or monuments? Which monument to go for? Are you able to get away with so little food production this turn? While this is enjoyable, these elements of fun are likely explored better in other games.

RATING: 4
A bit to abstract and infected with Eurogame sensibilities that ignore the importance of war and diplomacy amongst players. War is abstracted out to be "Disasters" but not mentioned as such. Bronze age Kingdoms did a lot of fighting and killing and conquering, as well as building and trading. The problem, of course, are the players who instead of building will go and attack and attack and attack to get their resources. So instead of coping with a mechanism to limit such gaming behavior they eliminate it altogether. Otherwise a nice dice game with nice bits that is a bit overpriced.

RATING: 8
TRADED IT! (for Ground Floor)

This was the one game to fall through the cracks of my rule; "Every 8 rated game I ever played, I now own." I believe I just played it too much early on, that I never needed to revisit it all these years later (and my copies faded dice always irked me).

The game really is one of the best "Yahtzee" style dice games out there. The wood board and pegs really add to it's classic feel. It does suffers from downtime with 3/4 players, but is otherwise an extremely solid design.

RATING: 8.5
A filler that's a civ game with dice? As long as you're not expecting a sumo wrestler the size of Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization, and judge the game on its own merits as a dice-rolling filler game, Roll Through the Ages has to be commended for offering a genuinely civilization experience packed into 30 minutes. There's a basic tech tree, there's interesting choices, there's tension, and there's a high fun factor. A big thumbs up!  
The game ends quickly, I recommend playing up to 5 developments with 4 players, 6 developments with 3 players, and 7 developments with 2 players.
See my comprehensive pictorial review here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/380167
Also don't miss the [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/375374]solitaire high score challenge[/url] - my highest score is 93.  My personal best for regular solitaire game is [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/3120183#3120183]74 points[/url].

Note also that the designer has released a free print-n-play expansion which tweaks some rules and adds some developments: [gameid=47389]
See my complete overview of this official variant here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/450175
Main changes:
● Game ends with 7 instead of 5 developments
● Four new developments (Preservation, Smithing, Shipping, Commerce)
● Adjustments to the cost and point value of five other developments
● Adjustments to the cost for monuments, which are all in play (even in 2-3 player games)
● Trading is standard

RATING: 7
Roll Through the Ages: The Bronze Age is a nice and light 2* to 4 players game about the development of ancient civilizations.

Being based on dice rolling it is influenced by the blind lady's whims, but there's no room for complaints: as games last around 15 minutes, any bad luck is just a good excuse to play one more game. And then one more, if it clicks for you, as it's a game that can be very satisfying and might produces the just-one-more-turn (game in this case) feeling typical of many heavier turn based strategy games (like Civilization, Masters of Orion, and so on).

At the beginning of the turn you roll your dice (and re-roll some, up to a maximum of two times) to see which resources (grain, workers, coins, goods, or disasters) are available. You invest your resources into cities (your engine, each city giving you one dice to roll), developments (which give bonuses and points) or monuments (which score you points), and the game is over at the end of the turn when a player has purchased his 5th development or all monuments have been built. I like this, as it allows the players to set the game's speed: you might try to close a game you're leading or lengthen one where you have some catching up to do.
Developments cost money, which you get from coins and by selling goods; most developments are worth the investment (in around 50 games I have taken all of them at least once, with granary being my least bought development) and either give you a bonus or protect you from bad effects (droughts, pestilence, revolts).

Many strategies are possible: you can focus on workers and monuments, or goods and developments, or a mix of the two. You can play aggressively (getting many disasters/workers and neglecting to feed your cities), or carefully (trying to avoid VP penalties); you can also try to roll pestilences to damage your opponent. Monuments give more points to the first who builds them, so "stealing" them from under someone else's nose might be worth a few penalty points (for not supplying your cities with food). Consequently, when more than two players focus on monuments, the game will end sooner and someone going for developments might have a slight advantage (as the others are battling for the same pool of points).

In conclusion, this is a nice and fast game with a good amount of strategy thrown in. Recommended as a light filler, avoid if you hate randomness.

*: The solo game didn't work for me. The dice remove a good deal of the puzzle-like appeal that solo games usually have, and the lack of interaction makes it completely insipid and dull.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

There are several aspects to setting up your civilization: feeding them, gathering resources, researching new technology, building monuments, and even potentially some occasional disasters.  It's a fairly engaging solo game as well.

RATING: 7
I would describe this game as a tactical version of Yahtzee. By rolling the dice you will build your own civilication. The choices you'll need to make during the game will determine what kind of civilication arises.

Luck will always play a part with Roll through the Ages because the game involves dices. But as a player you can influence the dices (you can choose how many dices you want to roll, by building, or you can develop the skill to re-roll one of the dices each turn). 

Roll through the Ages is a game which we play very often. The game is ideal to play in between games or as an ending of an evening. Also the game is very compact, perfect to take with you on vacation.

RATING: 5
This game is like Yahtzee made to new level. Although I won in this game, actual game result is very dependent on successful dice rolls. At the beginning of game just roll workers as fast as possible to get more dice and also food to feed your "dice". Then just buy certain developments which helps you a lot, for example, the one which gives +1 food per die and then one which gives +1 worker per worker die. Game is quite short and strategically, tactically not deep at all - there are not much options to do or tough decisions to make.
I would recommend this game for those, who like Yahtzee, but want something more deeper and not so random as Yahtzee.

RATING: 7
Not exactly an original concept, but seems pretty well-executed. The faces on the dice combined with the amount of re-rolling means you actually can set out to pursue a certain path to victory without getting harmed by chance too often. There are lots of options of what to buy and the choices are easily understood. I wish it could accomodate more players. It also does seem to have some odd pacing issues. It seems to drag early on and then quickly ramp up to breakneck speed with tons of buying for about one or two rounds and then ends. I'd really have to play more to get a better handle on it. Rating could go up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Despite my famous dislike for dice games, I like this one and want to play it more!  It's probably because it's a civilization-type game, and I do like that.  It gives me that "increasing population and developing civ advances" feel that I like so much, without the length of Advanced Civilization or Through the Ages.  Actually, this even blows Tempus out of the water because it has no stupid Conversion cards (or other game-changing randomness) -- yes, I know how silly that sounds given that the whole game is random, but at least there's a way to mitigate the bad effects by buying appropriate civilization technology.

This game is also similar to Ra the dice game because of the skulls (which are like suns and can't be re-rolled, and which cause disasters), and because of the monuments which are mostly just for VPs, not for useful benefits (other than the Great Wall).

There is a rule that is important and easy to miss -- if you sell any good to buy a civ tech, you have to spend ALL of that good (moving the marker back to zero).  This makes things much simpler in terms of calculating which goods to spend when buying tech.  Spoilage (dropping down to 6 goods) doesn't work this way; you can lose less than a whole row of goods if you choose.

As with most people, I think that 5 civ advances is too few, and causes the game to end too quickly.  7 might be a better number.  Or maybe it should be like Advanced Civ where a certain point total ends the game (but that would require too much calculation).

RATING: 6
Well... it's not bad, but I find myself comparing it unfavorably to the Ra dice game. That game has the great central scoring board, which is absolutely crucial to generating player interaction. Instead of following the opponents' rolls to see how they compare on the various tracks in Ra Dice, players are generally just watching their own little thing here, rarely taking an interest in each other's rolls. With individual score sheets, Roll Through the Ages feels very much like a solitaire exercise. Meanwhile, helpful upgrades like Medicine only lessen what interaction is there by making you immune to other players' actions. It's not bad by any means, and I like a dice game like this from time to time, but Ra Dice is just so much better.

RATING: 9
12-26-13
After many more plays from my previous comments, I've come to realize luck doesn't play as large of a factor as I thought before.  Sure, if someone gets exactly what they need all the time, they'll probably win.  But there's always a use for half the dice.  It's not as though you will get absolutely nothing if you roll badly.

The different ways to piece together developments is interesting along with the pacing that the other players can cause.  You can go for a big machine of high developments that get you an immense amount of points, but if someone else is content going for a quick sweep of low points/short game before you reach your goal, it's an interesting chain of decisions to take into account.  The utilization of goods is nifty and overall this is my favorite dice game.

Especially considering it's fun with a wide range of people, this is a solid game.  I still don't quite feel right giving it a 10 along my others, but this is at the top of my 9s.

RATING: 5.5
This game is pretty fun as a 1-player endeavor every once in a while: try to beat your top score in 10 turns. I've played this game with up to four people also, which (to be honest) was pretty awful. Too much downtime waiting for others to try and figure out which dice combination they would like to keep. There is basically one dice combination that effects other players and can be easily avoided with the Irrigation development... which everyone always develops and then the game has NO player interaction. It becomes a solitaire game where players compare scores at the end = dull. The game still went quickly... a bit too quickly, as one player always seemed to hit the 5 development goal within the first 5-7 turns, which usually ended the game on the 1st or 2nd players turn, allowing them one extra turn than players 3 and 4. I think this game is best as a 1- or 2-player game.

RATING: 2
The less sophisticated, shorter version of its bigger brother. While I haven't played the longer version, this dice-rolling version is mildly interesting. It's Yahtzee on steroids, I suppose. Roll some dice to get resources and workers, to turn them into developments and monuments. Developments help you fend off disasters which can occur with rolling the dice. Seems straightforward, but the 3P game ended far too quickly. Could it be a good filler? I don't know--I'm now intrigued to play its bigger brother soon.

Update (08/17/10): I am convinced that I should stay away from all "roll this and roll that" adaptations of good games. The abstraction and resulting "stupid-ification" annoys the hell out of me. I will never play this again. Downgrading to a '4'.

Update (1/26/11): Who am I kidding? Never going to play this again. It's a '2'.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
En breve: un juego de forzar tu suerte tirando dados con una temática de civilizaciones. Un Yahtzee con tema que une dos conceptos antagónicos: filler y juego de civilizaciones.

Durante la partida, cada jugador va anotando sus elecciones en una hoja a tal efecto. Se tira 1d6 especial por cada ciudad (se comienza el juego con 3 ciudades = 3 dados) y se puede relanzar dos veces más, aunque los dados que muestren una calavera no podrán lanzarse de nuevo. Obtener una calavera únicamente no es dañina, pero más de una puede llegar a serlo, lo que se traduce en pérdidas de PV´s por lo general. 

El resto de las caras de los dados muestran qué se obtiene: el trigo sirve para alimentar las ciudades (1 trigo x ciudad o -1PV por ciudad sin alimentar). Las monedas sirven para comprar descubrimientos durante tu turno (no se pueden guardar para turnos posteriores) y los recursos, de los cuales hay 5, sirven para comprar  progresos. Los progresos no son más que ventajas que permiten a los jugadores poderes especiales o saltarse las reglas en forma de excepción, además de algún PV. Por último, los trabajadores sirven para fundar nuevas ciudades y tirar más dados el próximo turno o bien, construir monumentos que dan PV´s

PROS: de algún modo, el juego es un sistema de juego, de modo que si cambias las características reflejadas en las hojas del juego, obtienes un juego distinto, algo que se hizo con la expansión "Late Bronze Age".  Con la suficiente dosis de estrategia para estar un pelín por encima de juegos como "Piko Piko" o "Risk Express". Versión en solitario en la que hay que conseguir la mayor puntuación en 10 turnos. Dados de madera pirograbados en bajorrelieve que confieren a los componentes un aire de primitivismo acorde con el tema.

CONS: Reglas de almacenamiento de recursos un pelín difíciles de entender. La expansión traducida fanmade es mejor que el original si se busca más profundidad. Sin la variante comercio, el juego carece de interacción. 

En definitiva, simplón y repetitivo... pero no olvidemos que es un filler para jugar haciendo tiempo hasta estar todos los jugadores en una velada o en una tarde lluviosa de domingo.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Roll Through the Ages is an entertaining light filler game.  It uses dice in a Yahtzee mechanic that may be allocated after being rolled to direct the advance of your civilization in one of many areas(on a pre-printed score sheet just like Yahtzee!)  The dice also can result in a disaster like a flood or a drought or famine that hurts your score.  Some of the disasters strike all the other players as well.  The game amounts to a race to the endgame conditions and maximization of scoring by wise dice allocation choices.  With dice being involved, there is a significant degree of luck in this game.  It must also be noted that there is extremely limited player interaction.  Overall, it is a fun, light game that plays in a short stretch of time.

I suppose if you were drunk, and tripping on 'shrooms, and developmentally challenged to begin with, it could be an interesting way to kill some time in a completely mindless way. It's nothing at all like Civ.  IT'S YAHTZEE.  You just. roll. dice.   Then roll again.  Then roll again.  Move pegs.  Check boxes.  Roll dice.  Kill self.

RATING: 10
Mar09: This one really does accomplish what it sets out to do.  It's a short game with plenty of interesting decisions.  Some of them even long term (well, 20 minutes long term).

There are different paths to try.  You can rush the monuments.  You can build waaay up to 6-7 cities.  You can sort of go development crazy.

This really is the fun thematic dice game I've been looking for.  For now, it's better than Can't Stop and Monopoly Express both.

As far as game length goes, I'm not yet crazy about the 5 development ending.  I know other people have played around with upping that limit.  I think I'd consider messing with just a flat turn limit.  It would take away some of the uncertainty about when the game will end, but I figure you'll still be struggling to complete exactly as much as you can anywho.

July09: Late Bronze Age Update

This game goes all the way to almost 10.  With 2 players, it probably is a 10.  With more players, I just don't think the trading does enough to keep it from being boring not on your turn.

Really though, the expansion is awesome.  It adds more depth, more strategic paths and just that little bit more game time.  You really feel like you can make a plan and ride it all the way to the finish line.

Mar15:

Returned to this after a few years away.  The base game is still bleh, but the "Late Bronze Age" is still awesome.  I'm bumping it up to a 10.

RATING: 6
Wow, I had heard good things but really this was better than I expected as I didn't expect much for a pure dice game.  It actually feels like a game, even though your just rolling dice.  

Nice and simple rules, though the dang goods on the board were a little tricky, but overall pretty easy to teach so suitable for kids, familys etc.  Having said that, I found that playing with gamers the game still kept us interested and its fairly short and doesn’t overall stay its welcome.  It feels like your choices will matter, though going for cities (extra dice) early seems obvious and likely a must do strategy so it takes a little of that away.  (getting guys is too powerful?)

I was surprised too how much the theme actually came out, you kind of have the tech tree effect there, you have to feed, etc.  Luck is not that big a factor I found either, for a dice game, you do get 2 rerolls if you need them and there are a couple other little ways to help.  One of the better dice games I’ve played, especially where rolling dice is the whole game and there are no other accompanying mechanics but I do wonder/think that an optimal strategy would present itself after a few games.

Initial Rating: 7.0 (April 2010)

It´s an ok game. At first I ranked it better as I liked the game much more before but nowadays I only play it to advance ranks in Yucata. It was one of the first games I played. The play time is generally short and you have a lot of options through the game, still I find the luck to be annoyingly high (it is a dice game after all). I think the game is great for beginners as the rules are very simple. My main problem with the game is that I found myself taking the same path game after game so for me it has little replayability.

The components are top notch. 

Current Rating: 5.0

[hr]
2012-11-06

Nothing has changed, not only I always take the same path, this path is clearly superior to other paths (I won’t name it here because I don’t want to spoil the game for you) so I have to downgrade its rating. If two or more players follow this path, it is luck that defines who wins.

It is a good idea, but the game is unbalanced.

I have played it a lot on Yucata because (nowadays) I usually win so it is an easy way to advance ranks, but this is not a game close to my heart. If it weren’t for Yucata, I would play this once a year if that.

RATING: 6
This might be my favorite of the Yahtzee-esque dice games. Which isn't to say I love it, but I do enjoy it and there seem to be some more interesting decisions than there are in King of Tokyo (for instance). I'm struck by the fact that I am almost always pretty happy with my first roll of the dice. The only reason I ever re-roll is if I need food or I rolled a coin and don't have enough money to buy a development. We'll see if that stays the same. I'm hoping it doesn't, just because the rolls seem more like card draws without that push-your-luck tension.

Update: All of the above holds true, but the more I play, the more I realize that dice games like this one just aren't for me.

RATING: 7
This game has rapidly become the "good, quick filler" game of choice.  Not much interaction except for Pestilience and beating out another player to one of the monuments.  After the first few plays, the player who built up his/her cities quickest always won, but more recently I have seen players win with just five cities, pushing the goods/coins to get to five developments first, before the monument/city builders can take advantage of their position.  Thus, there appear to be multiple paths to victory and this bodes well for replay value.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Very fun game, played both the board game and iPhone app. Love the mix of strategy and tactics. This is due to the core mechanic revolving around dice. You want to have a solid long-term strategy and go in every turn with a goal. However, if the dice are not rolling your way, you need to alter your plans short-term to at least maximize your current turn, since some elements don't carry over very well (goods, coins and skulls). Randomness increases the variety of viable strategies, since you have to balance between flexibility (the ability to leverage any dice rolls) and risk (specializing in a certain path and looking for a restricted set of rolls)

RATING: 7
I'm baffled by the existence of the game. 

Roll Through the Ages is preposterous on its face. It's a "civ game" in which you collect resources, grow your population, develop innovations such as agriculture and masonry, and complete ambitious "wonders" like the Pyramids. Games like this are usually thought of as "streamlined" if they clock in at 4 hours or less. RTtA takes 20 minutes. Yeah, laugh it off. Your turn also consists primarily on rolling a set of dice up to three times (guffaw). The thing is, the game packs a reasonable amount of strategy as each player races to complete the developments and the wonders. And race is the right word: what you've got here is another quirky income-to-VPs gear shifter. The goals are tired but the mechanics are fresh, and the throwback quality of the pegboard and scorecards didn't hurt. Don't pass this one up.

RATING: 7.5
The "goods" are a little confusing at first.  We muddled through it and verified with someone who had played it right that we were doing it correctly halfway through.

Good, but seems short.  Only 5 technology improvement.

Played a second time and it still just seems too short.  I don't see how one can avoid the strategy where you make a development each turn, pestilence the other players, and take the starvation points if you have to.  Odds are you'll only starve one or two cities.   Monuments seem almost never to get built.

I'd like to try some things like: up the number of developments,  if you stave a city too often it goes away, pestilence means you don't get goods, skulls only produce one good, or also a number of monuments must be built before the game ends (ie five developments x number of players + 3 monuments * number of players).  

More interaction between players would be nice and I suspect the trading rules + a longer game might encourage it.

RATING: 8
A good entry in the dice-roller genre. More and tougher choices to make than in, say, Yahtzee, and with a theme that's not just pasted on - it really does work, in its own way. A little heavy to bring out for total non-gamers - the resource storage and accumulation rules are a little tricky to get the hang of at first - but with a little patience could even work out for this crowd.

Like many games, this one ends just when it seems like it's finally moving forward. Ending through all monuments being built is much harder to achieve than one player acquiring five technologies. Easily fixed with house rules, of course, or by using the PnP "Late Bronze Age" expansion.

RATING: 5
Generally my wife has not been a fan of dice games---she tends to think they're too light strategically with not enough decisions.  While I had enjoyed [thing=35503][/thing], she was not a big fan.  Still, I was intrigued by [thing=37380][/thing] particularly due to the civilization theme.  We ended up getting this as one of our Secret Santa gifts and we gave it a try.

The components are nice, chunky wood bits and I like the peg boards.  However, the game didn't do much for us.  It was somewhat pleasant to roll the dice and to gradually tally up your pegs, but there feels like even less interaction here than in [thing=35503][/thing]. The game didn't feel tense enough for us and the starving and disaster mechanism didn't feel punishing enough.  My wife found the game boring, although she wasn't very disposed towards dice games in the first place.  I prefer [thing=35503][/thing] over this, as at least there is the central board that the players are competing together on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dice games are the new new thing in euro design, and they're pretty hard to do well. The crux of the issue is that gamers like a measure of control and meaningful decisions, while dice are about pure randomness and, if they aren't designed around carefully, can easily subvert your decisions, rendering them meaningless. That or else the dice are so lifeless and meaningless that they may as well not be there. While Roll Through the Ages doesn't completely solve these problems, it does find a decent balance among them, and aside from the classic Liar's Dice it is among my favorite dice games.

It compensates for damping down the luck of the dice (which it does by making dice faces results, not numbers) by adding on a couple of small but interesting side systems for developing technologies and building wonders. While there is one "whammy" result, it is balanced by granting a couple of resources, as well, and depending on how many of them you roll you can nerf the result, too. The one really negative thing that can happen is failing to roll workers in the early rounds, putting you behind the other players in dice. While falling behind 6 dice to 5 is not a big deal, being behind 5 to three is hard.

Player interaction exists, but is a little thin. The first player to build a wonder gets more points than the second, and one of the "whammies" affects your opponents instead of yourself, but that's about it. To maximize this, add players, but there's not much to do during other players' turns, so diminishing returns set in fast. I'd peg 3 as the sweet spot, with 2 playing well if you're okay with solitaire-ish play. At 30 minutes or so I can happily overlook the low interaction, though. I'd rather keep downtime to a minimum and play with 2 or 3.

While Roll Through the ages isn't in the same class as Ra and (balance issues aside) Saint Petersburg, it is a bit faster, so it has a nice niche in most eurogamers' collections, even if it is not a must-buy.

RATING: 8
This is a clear winner in terms of number of plays and I retain a fondness for it. Initially, we played it a lot, all player numbers, including some time on the solo game working out the best score possible. A year on, it retains its value. If you want a short game where you build stuff, make use of different special abilities, which you then use to make points, you have that here. The weakness of the game are the dice of course (you can't get around the randomness there) plus the feeling for some that it is over too fast. A determined player can end it quite quickly, before (as a friend put it) anyone else gets a chance to play. The free expansion fixes this, making it longer and less of a sprint, with additional developments to explore as well. Though it does then lose its quick game aspect somewhat, so the base game is sometimes what you want. A favorite.

RATING: 8
This cool game occupies a pretty unique spot on the landscape, about halfway between goofy press-your-luck dice games of the YAHTZEE variety and deeper Euro-style civilization builders. 

It's not for everyone - there's nothing especially novel about its mechanics of civ-building that hasn't been done a zillion times before, and if you hate dicefests it will probably drive you extra nuts. But for a lightweight Euro-fan like myself who has always found Yahtzee to be far and away the least objectionable amongst Stupefying Americam Mainstream Parlor Games (let's call them SAMAPS) it will hit quite a few sweet spots. Profoundly teachable, too.

RATING: 7
This is fun and highly addictive dice rolling game with a civilization theme.  You have to roll dice up to thee ties that can provide food, workers, money, trade goods, x2 trade goods with a disaster.  You use food to feed cities and workers to build more cities and great wonders.  You use trade goods to advance on tracks which when sold give you more money to buy technological advances, like leadership.  You want lots of trade goods but not too many in that you can get disasters.

I find the game very fun and there is a little interaction too in that some of the disasters affect other  players.  It has resource management and press your luck.  I like the game a lot and very fun solitaire as well.  The iPad app works well but does show its age.

RATING: 7
這是個有關建設古文明的擲骰遊戲dice game。遊戲中有7個獨特的遊戲骰，另有4塊木製的記錄板以記錄糧食food 及5種財貨(木材wood、石材stone、陶器pottery、布匹cloth、矛頭spearhead)。玩家輪流擲骰生產糧食及各種財貨、勞動力。勞動力用以建城市及奇觀。積累的財貨用以換取文化。建設古文明間，亦會迎來各種災難。
玩家在其記分表上記錄各自的歷史成就。遊戲結束時，總分最高者勝。

遊戲開始前，玩家分别拿取1張記分表及1枝鉛筆(自行準備)，並各取1個記錄板及6枝不同顏色的柱狀標記。6枝不同顏色的柱狀標記，其中除糧食food的位置是3外，其餘5枝分别代表木材wood、石材stone、陶器pottery、布匹cloth、矛頭spearhead，皆插在行列中最左方無數字的位置上。

任意選擇1名玩家開始遊戲。開始遊戲的玩家先在其記分表的角落劃上「星」號作記認，然後拿取3顆遊戲骰。

遊戲以回合進行。每回合玩家依順時針方方輪流進行1個玩家回合。所有玩家皆完成1個玩家回合後，1回合完結。

玩家在其玩家回合中，依次進行下列5個步驟：

1. 生產
玩家在記分表上每建有1個城市city，即擲1顆遊戲骰(開始時每人皆有3個城市)。
首次擲骰後，擲出骷髏的骰子必須保留並旁置，然後玩家可在餘下的骰子中保留任何骰子，而重擲其他骰子。玩家可選擇保留所有骰子而直接收取財貨。
重擲的骰子中，擲出骷髏的骰子必須旁置。然後玩家可在所有非骷髏的骰子(包括首次擲骰後保留的)中，選擇再重擲其中任何骰子。玩家可接受擲骰結果直接收取財貨。
再次重擲骰子後，玩家必須依擲骰結果收取財貨。

遊戲骰的6面分别代表不同結果。
3禾：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家在其記錄板最下方糧食food一列，將標記往右移3個位置(即多了3個糧食)。玩家的糧食達到15即不可再增加。
1壺：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家即收取1個財貨goods。財貨必須先計算總數，然後才決定種類(見下文)。
2壺+骷髏：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家即收取2個財貨goods。財貨必須先計算總數，然後才決定種類(見下文)。若多於1顆骰顯示這面，玩家即需於步驟2迎來災難。
3人：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家即可於步驟3中，在其記分表上的城市city或奇觀monument劃去3個格子。
2禾/2人：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家可選增加2個糧食，還是在步驟3中劃去2個格子。多於1顆骰顯示這面，玩家可分别揀選不同的選擇。
錢幣：每有1顆骰顯示這面，玩家即可於步驟4中，換取1項知識技術時其代價降低7。

玩家依擲骰的結果收取財貨時，必須計算所得財貨的總數，然後才依木材wood、石材stone、陶器pottery、布匹cloth、矛頭spearhead這個次序，由下至上再上至下反復逐一增加。
例：玩家拿到共3個財貨，即會得1木材、1石材、1陶器。
例：玩家拿到共6個財貨，即會得1木材、1石材、1陶器、1布匹、2矛頭。
玩家的財貨以標記所在位置代表。每取得某項財貨，該標記即往右移1個位置。若桌財貨的標記經已移至最右位置，玩家即失去該個財貨。標記下的數字，即該數量財貨的價值。

2. 災難
玩家每有1個城市city，即扣減1個糧食food。若玩家的糧食不足，每缺少1個糧食將扣減玩家1分。減分以記分表下方災難disasters一欄記錄，每扣減1分即劃去1個格子。

檢視玩家擲骰所得的骷髏總數，以決定災難的效果。
1個骷髏：無災難發生。
2個骷髏(旱災)：除非玩家擁有灌溉irrigation，否則扣減2分。
3個骷髏(瘟疫)：所有對手扣減3分，除非該玩家擁有醫藥medicine。
4個骷髏(蠻族)：除非玩家建有長城great wall，否則扣減4分。
5個或以上的骷髏(起義)：除非玩家擁有宗教religion，否則失去所有財貨good。若玩家擁有宗教，則所有對手失去所有財貨(除非該玩家同樣擁有宗教)。

3. 建設
依玩家擲骰所得的工人數(人仔)，玩家即可於其記分表上的城市city及奇觀monument中，任意劃去相同數目的格子。任何「2禾/2人」的骰子若已取得2個糧食，即不作工人論。
玩家若在1個城市city或奇觀monument上，劃去其上全部的格子，即完成該城市或奇觀。
完成1個城市，即以筆圈上該城市上方的骰子圖案。下回合玩家即多得1顆遊戲骰。
完成1個奇觀，即以筆圈上該奇觀的分數。所有玩家中首名建成該個奇觀者，取較大數字的分數；後來者取較小數字的分數。
例：甲共得5工人。甲花3工人成為首位建成金字塔(3格)，得1分(後來者將無分數)。餘下的2工人用於建城市，甲在其第4個城市(3格)劃去2格。

4. 文明
玩家可以財貨goods換取1項知識技術。記分表上成就developments一欄中列出共13項知識技術。最左方數字為其代價，跟著是名稱、分數及效果。
玩家擲骰所得的每個錢幣，皆會得到7點的折扣。
玩家若以某項財貨支付代價時，必須扣減所有該財貨。遊戲中不設找贖，超出的數值即會浪廢。玩家可以多項財貨支付代價。
例：甲有2木(價值3)並擲得2個錢幣(折扣14)。甲需全數扣減2木，方可換取農耕agriculture(代價15)。

5. 庫存
玩家最多只可保有6個財貨goods。若玩家有多於6個財貨，即必須自行選擇扣減那種財貨，直至只保有6個財貨。

遊戲持續至達成以下二個情況之一，則完成該回合後，遊戲便即完結。即記分表上劃有「星」號者右方的玩家，完成其最後的玩家回合後，遊戲便會完結。
a. 某1名玩家換取了5項知識技術。
b. 所有玩家的奇觀，每種奇觀皆已建成最少1個。

遊戲完結後，玩家即在其記分表上計算自己的總分。加上成就developments的分數、奇觀monuments的分數、知識的獎分bonuses的分數，再扣減災難disasters的減分，即玩家的總分。總分最多者勝。總分相同，則以其中財貨goods總值最多者勝。

知識技術
領導leadership：步驟1中完成擲骰後，可重擲1顆骰(骷髏亦可)。
灌溉irrigation：免受旱災影響。
農耕agriculture：每收取「3禾」或「2禾/2人」的糧食food時，多得1個糧食。
採石quarrying：生產時一旦得到石材stone，多得1個石材。
醫藥 medicine：免受瘟疫影響。
鑄幣 coinage：每個錢幣得到12點的折扣。
商隊 caravans：玩家略過步驟5，可保有任何數目的財貨goods。
宗教 religion：起義時將禍害推予他人。
糧倉 granaries：步驟4中，玩家可用任何數目的糧食food來支付代價。每個糧食的價值是4。
砌石 masonry：步驟3中，每顆骰提供工人，皆作多1工人論。例如以「2禾/2人」用作工人時，即當作3個工人。
工程 engineering：步驟3中，玩家可扣減任何數目的石材stone以作工人論。每1個石材作3個工人論。
建築 architecture：玩家每完成1個奇觀monument，額外多得1分獎分。
帝國 empire：玩家每有1個城市city，額外多得1分獎分(即3~7分)。

3人遊戲：
遊戲開始前，劃去記分表上奇觀monument中的空中花園hanging gardens(11格)。

2人遊戲：
遊戲開始前，劃去記分表上奇觀monument中的神殿temple(7格)及大金字塔great pyramd(15格)。

1人遊戲：
遊戲只進行10回合，玩家目標為挑戰最高得分。
災難中瘟疫將扣減玩家3分，除非玩家擁有藥物medicine。
步驟1中，容許玩家選擇重擲擲得骷髏的骰子。

交易：
玩家們可自決是否加入交易規則。在步驟2後加入交易時段，玩家可與其他玩家交易糧食food及財貨goods。其他玩家間不得進行交易。
玩家可自行決定交易內容及次序。例：先3木換2石；再以3石換2陶。

RATING: 6
For a long time I thought I wanted to own this, but after playing it that desire has gone. This is not a bad game - in fact, it's a perfectly serviceable dice game, but it's just that. The components are lovely to hold and look at, and conceptually the theme is cool, but they don't make a huge impact. It doesn't feel like I'm playing a civ game, it feels like I'm playing a Yahtzee variant. The dice are a bit bigger than is comfortable to roll too. The game is reasonably fun, and works fine as a filler, but (and maybe I'm spoiled here) I've come to expect that even filler games can offer a good amount of strategic depth, so I find this game somewhat lacking.

Update: Ended up buying the game anyway.

People who don't like light dice games.

RATING: 7
Simple dice-based civilization game for 1-4 players. 7 wooden dice are used as in Yahtzee to produce food, goods, workers and coins. Workers can build cities or monuments, goods and coins can be used to finance developments. Many of these provide special abilities as well as victory points. Progress and scoring is done on paper sheets provided with the game.

RATING: 6
Interesting game, but not overly more complex than say triple Yahtzee despite what all the "too good for yahtzee" haters would have you believe. You roll dice and try to shoot for certain faces to come up, you then deal with what you get and decide where to put the results. I do like it and it certainly is more complex than most dice games, but it's still just a dice game.

I think my biggest reason for not running out and buying it straight away is the price. It's pretty lavishly produced and thus expensive for "just a dice game". I dunno if it's really worth it.

RATING: 7
12/03/09 Obtained, second edition. Unlike some dice allocation games (e.g. Settlers of Catan dice game) that are nothing more than glorified Yahtzee, Roll Though the Ages has more than one path to victory, and there is player interaction. Different strategies can be developed and pursued. Most dice games play themselves because there's really only one way to win. The solitaire option for RttA is challenging, unlike most games that have one that normally feels just tacked on. Production standards are high (in my 2nd edition). I appreciate the thick pad of score sheets. 
As of 12/06/17 I've played 18 games, most were three or four player affairs. Two were two-player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Game in a Nutshell: Use dice to make for the fastest civ building diversion out there.

Played as: Solo, 2 player (2,3,4 player on Yucata.de)

Pros: LOVE the big chunky wood dice and player boards. Has a classic-ness to it while offering a different twist on the short dice game. Would make for a fine filler due to short play.

Cons: The game never fully gets over looking like a total cluster. Mechanically, it feels very spreadsheet-sh. Lacks player interaction. The first few rolls are too important... an awesome first couple turns can be tough to overcome for the opponents. There's less choice (the advancements aren't all that great) and more just doing the best with what's given to you. I wasn't impressed by the solo game.

Other: After enough plays, you begin to realize that it's more a fast shallow game than the quick civ simulation you were really hoping for. Haven't tried the advanced rules but my impression is it would only strip out what's still good in the game (the shortness) and just make it a longer random grind (there's other games I'd rather play in an hour time-slot). 

Best Use: Perfect for a quick, durable game on the road.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This may be the first civ-builder filler and it's quite fun.  A dice rolling game where you're rolling for food, money, goods & workers.  You can re-roll dice a couple of times but may need to face consequences if SKULLZ are rolled.  The cool thing is that that skulls provide some "goods" (which are traded for money to build on the technology track (powers) and some of those earned powers allow you to use the skulls against your opponents.

You need to feed your people, build temples, build cities (for more dice), etc.  As with most of these types of games in the end it's a pretty good idea to try to stick to a strategy just be aware that the dice may change that strategy and a little modification may be necessary.

Also, for a small game I thought the production was quite good. The pegboards were a great concept for keeping track of food and goods.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A dice-based game wherein players roll dice to get resources, and then use those resources to either build cities (to get more dice when rolling), build buildings (to get special powers), or build monuments (to get points).

While that description makes it sound fun, and much like other euro-games I like, this game is _way_ too random.  While you do get to re-roll some dice a few times, it just ends up being a fest of "who can get lucky first".  

I'm sure there's strategy, and I'm sure there's ways to mitigate the luck, but I don't want to bother to spend the time learning them.  Because even after I do, it'll still all come down to a dice roll.

And if I'm going to roll this many dice in a game, I'd rather be playing something like Battleground: Fantasy Warfare or Conflict of Heroes.  If I want a euro-game, I want to be able to control it some!

Bah.

RATING: 5
Roll dice to improve your civilization for most points.

Plays: 2.
Type: Dice game = a game where you roll lots of dice.
Complexity: Medium dice game.
# of players: See BGG.
Playing time: See BGG.
Player interaction: Competition for unlimited resources - monuments and some dice results that can hurt other players.
Randomness: High - Dice.

Replayability: Average, it's very quick so why not play again?
Fun: A bit, when dice go your way.
Memorability: Not really, it's a dry experience and quickly over.

Thematic integration: Weak.
Components: Nice dice. The need for pen-and-paper scoring is a bit fiddly.

Who will like this: Meh. I don't imagine anyone feeling strongly about this either way.

RATING: 7
This was a pleasant surprise, though I absolutely would not want to play with more than two people--the downtime would be terrible. There's some nice stuff to consider, and the game actually seems to have some strategy to it. All in all, pleased with this one as a leisurely pastime.

[b]Update:[/b] After about a dozen plays, Roll Through the Ages started to just seem like the same game over and over. But we kept plugging away, and I have to say it's really grown on me. Experienced players should trounce new ones, so this is not as much of a luck fest as it might seem, and--with two players at least--there's been an increasing amount of interactivity. Some moves seem stronger than others, but once both players realize that, you have to start coming up with new strategies to pull of the win. I'm not a big fan of resource management games, but Roll Through the Ages keeps things light and quick while providing a decent amount of depth. I'm surprised to be saying it, but I think this one's a keeper!

RATING: 7.5
A dice roller to simulate a mini-civ building game. This one is very clever and comes complete with great components.

The game revolves around 5 key features...goods, population, coins, food and wonders/cities. Population is needed to build new cities and wonders of the world (worth VPs). Cities are vital as they allow more dice to be acquired and rolled each turn but cities require food so immediately there is a conflict on what should be kept each turn. Goods and coins are needed to develop new advances, which in turn offer a player a key benefit as well as some victory points. 

But all of this rolling comes with some risk as Skulls represent hazards and disasters. Some of these can be negated by developing the right advancement.

The backdrop to all this is that the game will come to an end when a certain number of Wonders are built, sot he clock is always ticking...and rarely does a player complete all that they set out too.

This is the only real complaint for some people. That the game ends just as it gets going. As a filler I think it is fine but the Late Bronze Age mod apparently fixes this issue to some degree.

RATING: 7
In this Civilization themed dice rolling game, each player gets a scorepad showing their cities, developments, monuments,  and disasters. Roll dice to get resources to grow your economy, such as earning more dice, get more storage, get more bonuses for building and so forth. 

The components are nice, and the game clocks in under 45 minutes. It has a fair share of luck, and there are various strategies to try each time you play. Games are typically quite short, and over in about six or seven turns (one of the game ending mechanisms is the player to build 5 developments).

It's fun, pretty quick and doesn't require a lot of exhaustive thinking. This game has slowly grown on me over time and despite the luck, there are various things one can try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Despite the title and artwork, Roll Through the Ages has got absolutely nothing to do with the heaviest Eurogame in town (Through the Ages): it's got a historical topic with players building monuments and achieving developments, but it's basically a push-your-luck resource management game with some room for dirty play (which is always welcome).

Players have got to score points throwing dice one to three times each turn and milking the throws the most: via goods used to gain developments, builders to get extra dice (cities) or construct monuments, food to "feed the dice" every turn and skulls to keep players on their toes (if the skulls are three it will be their opponents who are on their toes). There's a considerable amount of information involved in the whole palette of developments for the actual difficulty of the game but overall it plays quickly, easily and in a rather endearing way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Looked a little bit thin at first, and we were wondering what it actually had to offer that Pocket Civ didn't. But, as it turns out, it's an engaging if hardly intellectual exercise, and a system that seems to be set up nicely for future expansions. The luck quotient is far less than in some games that don't even require dice. Playing with the optional trading gives it a Catan-esque bite, too.

Further Thoughts: the web-published expansion is the key to keeping this one alive. Print, laminate and enjoy.

RATING: 9
I really like the wooden dice.  For one thing they're attractive; for another, they force the player to weigh the value of each roll against the possibility of a superior result from rolling again.  This is a little stressful for cautious types like myself, but also exciting.  There are actually lots of decisions to be made on each turn, and things move fairly quickly in this game, even with four players.  

The wooden pegboard for keeping track of goods and food is very nice, although the pegs themselves are a little too thin and therefore easily dislodged.  The score sheets are nicely formatted and scoring is refreshingly straightforward. 

The fact that this game can even be played solo puts it high on my list of games to purchase.(5+4)

RATING: 7
Roll symbolled dice to get food (to keep your VP’s), workers (buy more dice or go for VP monuments) and resources (to buy tech tree powers also worth VP’s). You get to actively choose what to pursue with your two re-rolls based on what you’ve bought so far and what your opponents are doing, all recorded via personal score pads. There’s some nice tech tree combinations to shoot for, some engine-building vs VP trade-offs, and races to complete things first - all of which makes for an engaging game. It has the added advantage of tailoring length (using the Late Bronze Age rules) and interaction (using the optional trading element). Repeated play seems to show that the tech tree is unbalanced (protection against pestilence seems powerful) which has diminished the allure somewhat.

RATING: 8

What I like:
- There are a surprising number of tough decisions (both strategic and tactical)
- Rolling dice is always fun
- Solid wood components in a small box

What I don't like:
- Managing goods is confusing at first
- Game can end too soon
- Using pencil and paper to track progress

Overall, a solid dice game that is light enough for casual gamers but interesting enough for medium gamers too. It requires more thinking than Ra Dice. I think the weight of 1.8 is about right.

RATING: 6
Be warned: this light Yahtzee-ish dice game has nothing in common with the game "Through the Ages" except the box art.  In this game, you got to roll dice to collect money (or resources that could be converted to money) to buy advancements that gave you special abilities and were worth victory points, or you could collect people, which either let you roll more dice or build monuments worth points.  You were allowed to re-roll your dice, unless it came up as a disaster which must be kept.  The severity of the disaster depends on how many disaster icons you rolled.  This game was pretty random, and I wish it didn't end so suddenly.

(5+ plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A city-building type of game using a dice rolling & re-rolling system similar to that seen in Yahtzee and other games. Points are scored based on your choices of converting the dice results into food, goods, money, workers, etc. and building various monuments & cities.

The game plays really quickly and there appear to be plenty of options for trying out different strategies.

Components (1st Edition) are definitely not up to the quality I was expecting. Some of the symbols on some of the dice are barely visible. The holes in the peg boards appear to be hand-drilled, with mistakes and partially-drilled holes in some places - and there are scuffs which have taken off the (important!) numbers under some of the symbols.

OWN: 1st Edition.

RATING: 2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Quick yahtzee type game. It doesn't really feel like you're building a civilization at all, and at the same time it does require the bookkeeping and calculations and resources and food and workers that you find in civilization building games.

There's a little bit of interaction, a little bit of strategy, but it's mostly rolling dice. It's not bad; rolling dice is fun, and trying to roll the skulls to get a plague on the other players instead of yourself, or trying to snipe a wonder from someone else gets he attention of the rest of the players in an otherwise solitaire game. You can make some nice combos with your developments, but the game will be over before someone can really take advantage.

One problem I see with Roll Through the Ages is that it's balanced to the point where you don't very much care what you roll.

Not a bad filler, but I prefer King of Tokyo in this type of game. Better integrated theme, far more interaction, simpler (less bookkeeping), more variation between games, and it's more honest about being a luck-based dice fest. It also feel like there's more on the line when you roll your dice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played numerous solo games, and finally played one face to face, almost forgetting that skulls rolled cannont be rerolled; just goes to show that even with a simple ruleset things can be overlooked if not played often enough (or it's a sign of dementia).

Marginally better than the Settlers dice game (though not as exciting as I employ a running score rule in Settlers) in that RttA has a better narrative and interesting ways to improve on die rolls (note To Court a King that improving is better than modifying, as it doesn't rob you of the tension of pushing your luck).

One thing the Settlers dice game has over RttA is the visual nature of the game compared to the wordiness of RttA. It is interesting to see how for some players (especially mentally drained) how difficult to see the profitable avenues and so deciding what to keep and reroll.

As with most dice games suffers from spectator issues, with the exception of 3 skulls rolled changing passivity to active non-player interest.

Update: played another face to face game and again found the play interesting, but not exciting or fun. I think the problem is that despite the strong theme of building it is a 'left brain' experience. It is difficult to 'see' the best avenue of progress because the process of bookkeeping seems to be at the fore rather than comparing the status which is essential to know in order to then determine where to get ahead: you then have goals to go for and so can get excited about the die rolls. This is where the Settlers dice game succeeds.

I believe the game is better if one modifies the rules a bit - the game as it stands is over far too quickly. We often play that the game ends when one player has 7 techs (vrs 5) or when all of the monuments have been built AND one player has at least 3 of these (not necessarily the first place monument but has at least 3). This seems to add just enough time to make the game a bit meatier without overstaying its welcome.

RATING: 6
It sure looks pretty but for that price, all you get is a very sophisticated Yahtzee clone (albeit better than King of Tokyo). The interaction is limited and there is not much you can do to hinder, let alone attack opponents. Sure, some of you dice rolls might result in minus points for your opponents, but the problem is that you would never make a strategic decision to do so but rather accept that fact if you should happen to roll the dice that cause minus points for you enemies and as such, it is as random as the rest of the game. At least, there are two levels of strategy, push-your-luck when rolling the dice and some decision make as to where to allocate the workers/money. An okay Yahtzee clone that is not interactive enough to make it shine.

RATING: 8
Roll Through the Ages encapsulates all of the things I love about civ games and shrinks them down into, well, a capsule.  I love that this game takes twenty minutes to play!  For the most part you can really plan out almost exactly what you'd like to do, but the chance of rolling skulls (and sometimes you want skulls) creates enough randomness to make the game interesting.  This game utilizes one of my favorite game attributes which is to force players to work with what they have to the best of their abilities. Besides, the pieces are neat. I didn't have any real component complaints with my game.  The dice are too big for my tiny hands though.

RATING: 5
A slickly themed and produced, but not very interesting, dice game. It definitely works and looks good, but doesn't really scratch any itch for me. Maybe because it is rare than any roll is a total disaster, it lacks the drama of some other dice games. There are some decisions about long vs short-term strategies, and risk-taking on the big monuments, but the rest is largely turn by turn points optimization. Possibly as good as a dice game of this sort can be (better than Settler's Dice Game for instance) but that is not a high bar in my book.
