RATING: 7.7
This is a great quick dice game that I would call a strategic yahtzee.  Plays solo in about 20 minutes.  is a dice game but the dice rolls aren't so overpowered with luck because there is always some thing that can be done with the dice.  I believe there is a real number crunching game of probabilities with this game but it doesn't have to be that way to be enjoyed.  I think it's a game that can be fun for analyzers and casual players.  Played three times solo and improved all three game thus proving to me that there is a good learning curve and the dice rolls don't always have to dominant the outcome but rather how one uses the dice is more important.  Pleasantly surprised by this game

RATING: 7.7
It´s a very simple dice game, agile, fresh, addictive, quick, but with more strategy than you can think at first sight. I love their pyrographically engraved dices, and the thick wooden boards of resources: they give a primitive touch that fits very well with the theme. I think it's a great production design success, but it makes final product expensive, of course. Perhaps the paper pads should have been better, graphically speaking, or made of papyrus paper, for instance: that way could be more in line with the whole set (I don´t know if you know what I mean...). These pads are quite dull, and I'm thinking of making something handmade at home to replace them. Anyway, my wife and me really liked this one, and we recommend it. It´s a good dice game.

RATING: 6
Visual Appeal: (3)
This is an attractive and easy to decipher filler even if it looks like a history nerd's Yahtzee. Not much to look at really.

Components: (5)
The box is a great size for a filler game. It easily fits into a small bag for transportation. The pieces are all durable wood. The dice could be a little heavier, but they roll well enough. Paper score sheets are provided and they are plentiful. you can even print more online for free in addition to a "Late Bronze Age" variant. Love the game support! 

Theme: (3)
The game is a press your luck mechanism thinly veiled in the lightest civ game you will ever see. There's really a decent amount of flavor in the technologies and buildings that you produce, but I like themes to pop a little more. This game has it, but it still feels like roll, score, repeat to me.

Learning: (4)
It is a pretty simple game to learn. Players usually get a handle of the odds on the dice pretty quickly. The technologies are pretty self explanatory. Just remember to feed your cities!

Gameplay: (3)
The game goes by rather quickly. Balancing when to take people over money takes some practice, but it's a very calculate-able game. The game doesn't really promise much interaction, so I can't really hold it accountable. It provides just a little. I think there is one technology that allows you to affect your opponent with skulls on your die. Also completing buildings first will make them worth fewer VP to other players.

Overall Fun: (3)
There is some challenge to the game, but it quickly grows repetitive as you can only maximize your points in 1 or 2 ways depending on how the die come out. I think that in my usual groups, a press your luck game like "Zombie Dice" would do better for it's humor and taunt-friendliness.

RATING: 6
There's a few different strategies you can employ when playing this, which keeps it interesting. One way to play this though is fast - these roll again dice mechanisms really seem to fall flat with more than three players. In its basic form it's not far off being a solitaire dice game. The luck of calamities has a significant impact that is exacerbated by the shortness of the game - before you know it the game is over and you've blown your chances by trying to counter the calamities with your developments. Much like life, but that doesn't necessarily translate into a very positive gaming experience.

The small box and game footprint would seem well suited to taking this game to play on a plane, or just travelling in general.

The Late Bronze Age expansion adds real value to this game for me. Turns it into a game I quite like and my rating for the base game reflects this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is civilization as a light dice game. Each player gets a scoresheet where they record their choices. It does not bear comparison with Yahtzee. You roll dice each turn, one die per number of cities in your empire. You can reroll dice up to three times, with the exception that you can't reroll a skull. One skull is not harmful, but two or more have different disaster effects, usually negative score points. You roll wheat to feed your empire, which needs one wheat per city or you suffer starvation (another disaster effect). Roll a gold which lets you buy advancements this turn (unused gold is lost). There are five goods you can accumulate of different rarity, and a cribbage-like board for marking your stored goods and wheat.  Goods can be sold as sets to buy the more expensive advancements. Advancements change the rules of the game, offering you bonus score points, cheaper construction, or improve the effectiveness of various die roll results. Roll workers (again, use it or lose it) to invest in monuments and new cities. The larger your empire gets, the more likely you will suffer disaster effects, but there are advancements to mitigate most of then.

I enjoyed the game because your dice can be used in so many ways with different trade offs, but your turns are still very quick. The game ends after someone buys a fifth advancement. Your score at the end is the sum of your advancement points, monument points and bonuses for monuments and cities, minus your disaster points. There are several paths you can take to victory. If the dice aren't cooperating, you can try a different path. A nice quick civ on dice.

RATING: 1.5
Own this because one of the staff at Compleat strategist in NY convinced my friend "it's the same as TTA". I'm still pissed off...

The game has for me the least attractive components of any game I own. I'll take plastic over wood any day. Plus it's dry as a desert and it's a MPS. 

[u]EDIT: Played with The Late Bronze Age expansion and it's much better.[/u] 
First of all: the monuments and goods have NAMES! (Which genius forgot to include them in the first place!?) 
Second: More stuff to do, making the game come to its full fruition as you can pursue a specific path a couple of turns
Third: Obligatory trading phase may not be much, but you actually look at other player's boards. What a relief that is. 
So all in all expansion feels like the only way to play this game (I call it "designer's cut"). 

With that it's a 6. Dropped 0.5 point for base game. Go expansion!

RATING: 7
Light and fluffy 'yahtzee' type game. Players roll dice in order to generate workers, money, food, goods in order to build cities (more dice), monuments (VPs), developments (VPs & special abilities). Failing to get food will result in -ve points.

Essentially multiplayer solitaire but still great as a filler. Would have been great to have doubled or tripled the amount of dice to other players can take their turns while opponents are allocating results.

Overly generous players' pad adds weight to the box.

RATING: 6

It's an enjoyable half-hour-or-so experience and FRED's done a nice job with the little wooden peg boards, although the wooden dice are just okay.

RATING: 6.5
I have to admit I'm really impressed with how much game there is in this package. A few dice, a wooden board, some pegs and a sheet of paper are enough to create a feeling of developing your civilisation. It's not the deepest game out there, what you need to do each turn is pretty straightforward and due to the playing time, there isn't much gravity to your choices. On the other hand, how many board games give you the opportunity to develop a civilisation during a lunch break?

The solo aspect of the game works as a clever puzzle, giving you 10 turns to rack up as many points as possible. Thoroughly enjoyable, compact, easy to explain and with a small space requirement, this game is ideal for travelling on a train. Well worth the price.

RATING: 6.5
This game is much more fun if you don't care what happens.  It's a light filler, and as long as you don't expect anything with depth, and treat it solely as a fun little dice game.

This is less a standard dice game and something more along the lines of games were your actions are determined by dice (Minos, War of the Ring).  In those two games in particular, you have a board and a number of options that are allowed to you once you have rolled certain kinds of actions.  Here everything is abstracted out to where the dice dictate your whole game.  

You roll wheat you can feed your people, if you don't you starve.  If you don't get goods, then you can't get discoveries, no people equals no cities or monuments.  Now in a likely scenario all things even out over time.  Unfortunately, this game is too short to do that.  So you roll to see what happens and you make some minor decisions with how you allot your fate.

I do mind the rolling penalties for everyone but the player that rolled them.  Those diseases (or whatever they are called) can stack up quick and effectively eliminate you without any chance of protecting yourself.  If you were never able to get medicine, then you can easily lose 10 or more points.  This wipes out any sense of multiple paths to victory.  But, at least it gives a choice to the player that is stuck with two of the death's heads.  Do they reroll something that they really need to screw other players (and get more goods).

While it is cool that they did what they did in a dice game, I feel like many of the decisions you make are all just attempts to see what happens.  This is fine in an experience game, but there isn't a whole lot of experience here.

All in all it functions, and is quick and harmless.  A fun enough closer.  But, a bit of a disappointment considering the name that it is attached to.  But what was I to expect?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Actually, it is good. In fact, it may be the best of the complicated-Yahtzee-variant genre -- it works well, AND it has a way-cool theme! And I quite like the production, wooden dice and all! I ended up playing this 5 more times later on (a nickel already!).

The game is simple: start with 3 cities. Each city = 1 d6 (specially marked). You get 3 rerolls; can reroll anything except the disaster tiles. Collect food and resources rolled. Then feed cities (1 food each) -- each deficiency = -1 point. Finally, use workers to build cities (dice) or monuments (VP), and coins/resources to buy developments (special powers and VP). Disaster rolls may affect you or other players (more negative points; possibly lose all your goods). The game ends at the end of the round when either someone buys their 5th development, or all monuments have been built by some player(s). Most VP wins.

RATING: 7
[Gryphon Games 09 edition] {Civilization; dice-rolling; optimal 2-4} {Standard 6.5; Trading 7} Roll through the Ages is a remote Yahtzee variant with a civilization theme.  The theme is quite well executed; thus, it is easy to explain the game.  Unlike Yahtzee, it is not entirely a multi-solitaire dice game because player can influence when the game ends.  This creates a tension about game end and players have to find ways to maximize their scores on account of this.  This game is neither novel nor great but it suits for civilization fans who are looking for meaty game for around 15-25 minutes.

The choices offer enough tactical decisions for players, yet not taxing.   Players have to maintain a good balance in order to get high score.   Monuments offers attractive score but they do not provide additional tactical value.  Development offers both scores and strategic planning, which are more important in early games.  It's better to collect goods in the game too so as to accumulate attractive price.  Cities offer no immediate score but they provide important additional dice so that players can get more goods / foods / workers in a round; nonetheless, they had to be fed.  So, there are enough meaning choices to make this game a meaty filler.

The production is of high quality.  Wooden components are usually attractive.  The boards are sturdy and effective.  However, the dice are just so hard to read.  And, it's quite expensive!

RATING: 5
This was not my cup of tea. I know many people have been enjoying Roll Through the Ages as a fast dice filler, like Yahtzee but I suppose with a bit more going for it. I found the game to overstay its welcome, even though it didn't last very long, but still somehow managed to get boring and repetitive before it was over. The disaster mechanism, a staple of all civilization-themed games it seems, seemed flawed to me, as it randomly and significantly hurt yourself sometimes and your opponents other times. The components were large and chunky, but still disappointing because the dice were printed so faintly as to be very difficult to read. All in all, one of the biggest misses for me of the year. If I'm in the mood for a fast dice filler then I'd much rather play Sid Sackson's excellent Can't Stop.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A quite interesting step foward from the typical Yahtzee game: here, you also want to get types of combo rolls, but every face of the dice has use, being to get workers, goods, coins and food - the skulls can cause disasters (drought, pestilence, invasion and revolt), but also gives two goods.

In the game you have 2 main decisions after the dice stop rolling: where to use the workers and which developments to purchase. Sure, sometimes the decision is clear, being limited by the amount of goods + coins or because one big monuments is being built, and all the workers will go there, but usually the game gives you a nice set of choices.

The playtime is short - 30 to 45 minutes, with 4 players, less than 30 with 2 players.

I do recommend the variant: [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgameexpansion/47389/roll-through-the-ages-the-late-bronze-age]Roll Through the Ages: The Late Bronze Age[/url], the game gets better, with more options and a longer playtime that allows other strategies to be used.

My review (in portuguese) is [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/938579/roll-through-the-ages-the-bronze-age-resenha-em]here[/url].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is way too random and short. If you even once have a major food shortfall or four-skull disaster you're probably out of it.

I played with just the basic game, meaning player interaction is mostly semi-accidental (rolling disasters that affect others) or building a monument before somebody else can.

There's an impressive list of developments/technologies to research, but I think that a handful of them are clearly better than the others.

The dice are too big and the print on them is often faint.

The game end is triggered by someone getting their fifth development, or all monuments being completed. You can completely calculate who is in the lead. So, if you're not in the lead, you may feel obligated to just dump workers or money because spending them would mean ending the game in your own loss. Or else you can just concede... As it can be quite calculable that you have no way of catching up.

...Played again but with the Late Bronze Age free expansion. Opinion not really changed. Got to love how you can roll three skulls, nailing the other players, [i]and you get six goods[/i] because the system is too stupid to tell whether you deserve compensation.

The game kind of reminds me of Race for the Galaxy, which I also don't like: Mostly solitaire, and you try to figure out what to keep vs. what to reroll in order to navigate the spreadsheet as efficiently as possible. I do prefer this to Race, I think, since there's only six die faces to get comfortable with rather than a whole deck of cards. On the other hand Race seems less random and more likely to see a better player win.

RATING: 6
Play tested the game at Origins 2008.  Played once on Wednesday and again on Saturday.  Both times with the same rules even though some comments left me thinking they were changing some based on feedback.  I like the idea behind this game - a quick civ-like filler game.  The dice don't bother me either.
However, I don't really see much push-your-luck since re-rolling doesn't really have much downside.  Also, there was no trading and the only interaction between the players is an occissional disaster or making sure you start a different wonder.  Hopefully they'll tweak the rules as we discussed and the way I thought we were going to play on Saturday.  Without some interaction, I don't see the game being much fun besides being a solitary exercise in maxing points.
Obviously the base game didn't change the interaction.  I have never seen a race for 5 developments done properly lose a game to someone trying another strategy.  I'll play the game, but can think of better ones for a filler.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Zivilisationsspiele dauern in der Regel mehrere Stunden, wenn nicht gar Tage. Chvatils "Im Wandel der Zeiten" schafft es auf sehr galante Weise solch ein Spiel in einen Zeitrahmen von 3 - 4 Stunden zu pressen, was jedoch immer noch sehr viel Zeit ist.
Mit Im Wandel der Zeiten: Das Bronzezeitalter liegt nun der Versuch vor, das Spielgefühl eine Zivilsation zu führen und aufzubauen in ein Würfelspiel mit 20 Minuten Spieldauer zu pressen. 
Nach der sehr negativen Erfahrung beim Siedler-Würfelspiel stellt sich natürlich die Frage ob das überhaupt gelingen kann, oder ob dies wieder vollkommen in die Hose geht.
Die Antwort lautet kurz und gut: Es klappt. Es klappt sogar so gut, dass IWdZ als Würfelspiel sogar die Kriterien eines Spiel des Jahres erfüllen würde. Sehr eingänglich, familientauglich, mit Spielern jeden Alters problemlos spielbar, nicht zu komplex, aber auch nicht zu einfach. Dieses Spiel könnte Problemlos eine Oma mit ihren Enkeln spielen, ohne dass sich einer von beiden überfordert fühlt. 
Ich bni generell gar kein Fan von Würfelspielen, aber IWdZ macht einfach alles richtig. Man kann bis zu dreimal Würfeln, um das Ergebnis herbeizführen dass man gerne hätte. Seblst die negativ Ereignisse beim Würfeln ,die eigentlich Katastrophen für die eigene Zivilisation herbeiführen würden, sind gar nicht so tragisch, da man immer hoft, vlt. dann doch noch eine mehr zu Würfeln, weil Katastrophen viele Waren bringen und viele Katastrophen bei einer ausreichenden Anzahl an Würfeln nicht einen selbst, sondern die Mitspieler treffen.

Die Aufmachung ist wie gewohnt garndios. Gespielt wird auf sehr robusten und schicken Holztafeln auf denen mit schicken Markern der Warenbestand festgehalten wird. Der Fortschritt der eigenen Zivilisation wird auf einem doppelseitig benutzbaren DIN-A4 Spielblock festgehalten.

RATING: 5.5
Rating based on play with the Late Bronze Age expansion.  This is free and easy to print and there is no reason why anyone shouldn't use this expansion.  I see it more as a fix/errata than expansion anyway.  Upping the end game condition to 7 developments makes this game the perfect length.  Ending with 5 developments as per the original rules made the game finish just when your engine started to pick up speed robbing you of all the fun.

This is a fast and light game that gets pulled out frequently and is awlays enjoyed.

I'm not usually a fan of luck dependent games but I really dig the implementation of Roll Through the Ages.  It is very hard to get a bad roll.  Most rolls are useful and while they may not be optimal you can use them to your advantage by shifting strategies.

The game allows for some strategic planning (basically choosing which development combos you think you want to use at the start) but mostly involves a lot of tactics in reacting to what the dice end up rolling.

The developments definitely add to the replayability and I really enjoy the engine building and combos that you can get going with some of the developments.

RATING: 5
I really love the components of this game, it always gets "ooh's" and "aww's" when people see the nice, wooden dice and player-boards.

I got this game to fill-in for King of Tokyo when there's only 1 to 3 players, and it works well for that.  There's a very similar roll mechanic where you're shooting for different combo's on any given turn.

Where I'm a bit disappointed is the lack of thrill you normally get from dice-games.  Sure there's disappointment when you get that second skull and can't roll a third, but other than that, there's almost too many options to utilize dice, and so many points, it never feels like your back is against the wall, or you're hedging your bets on one lucky roll.

RATING: 8.5
PERMANENT PLACE IN COLLECTION

UPDATE: After 9 plays, upgraded to 8.5. I love this game. It's got nice bits, it's fast, it's strategic. Nice filler.

* A nice filler game with lots of depth. Brilliant fun solo on the iPhone, which is why I bought the physical version.

The wooden pieces are attractive and easy to transport. Would be good on a train.

Unfortunately, I didn't enjoy the physical game as much as the iPhone version. It's a math-out with no computer to help do the sums. Lots of calculating optimum strategies with the dice rolled. The effect of luck over the short game time is much lower than you'd expect for a dice game.

If you like accountancy, it's a 9 or 10. As I play the physical game two-player with my husband (who can do complex stats in his head), it's a 6 for me... I lose... And lose... And lose... Won't suit all playing groups.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I really dig this game.  It's a resource management/civ game that uses dice as a primary mechanism, but it's not strictly a dice game.  Considering that several turns in the first game involved one player spending about 5 minutes deciding what to do with his resources speaks volumes toward this game's possibilities, choices, and depth.  The interactivity, though slim, is enough to keep players from getting into an "untouchable" state, and the endgame condition of building 5 developments means that you can try different strategies between games.  The rules seem a little dense, and leave more questions than answers, but one time through the game answers a lot of those questions.  The solitaire game is interesting, if a little repetitive, and the game really meets its goals more with multiple players.  Overall, though, it's pretty impressive, and I look forward to more plays.

11 plays: 1 player
5 plays: 3 players
4 plays: 4 players

RATING: N/A
Players roll dice to obtain commodities and workers to build up their civilizations. Dice can be rerolled twice unless they come up as a hazard. Players use their workers to build infrastructure to support additional works or to build monuments that are worth points. At the same time commodities are gathered which allow your civilization to develop. Once all monuments or five developments are achieved by a player the game ends at the end of the round, points are counted, and a victor is declared.

RATING: 7
[i]Roll Through the Ages[/i] lets players be as risky as they want to be, adjusting the pace of the game to their playing style and letting them try to outrun the wave of negative points that often accompanies huge hauls of goods. You can bow out of the development race to some degree to focus on city- and monument-building, but by doing so you’ll waste any coins that you roll because you either can’t afford any developments or you’ve purchased all of the cheap ones on earlier turns. As in so many other games, a balanced approach is required for success.

For my [url=http://www.boardgamenews.com/index.php/boardgamenews/comments/game_preview_review_roll_through_the_ages/]complete review[/url], head to Boardgame News.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm very impressed with this game.  It is NOT Yahtzee masquerading as a civilization game.  Roll Through the Ages really does feel as if you are building TOWARD something, albeit in an abstracted manner.  

The various advancements that your civilization is racing to complete are mitigated by the food you need to grow for your people.  So a balancing act follows whatever game strategy you employ.

I also enjoy the need to watch how your opponents are building their particular civilization as it impacts the avenues open to your own.  Very fun game.

The components are fantastic.  Small wooden boards with wooden pegs fit perfectly together.  A boatload of scoring sheets shows that the publisher cares about boxing up a quality game, and the dice are so large you could use them as weapons!  

The box is compact enough to take anywhere.  Every house--gaming or not--should own a copy.  Rainy day?  Set it on the table.  Vacationing?  Suitcase it without worrying about losing any room to pack other items.  Heading to a coffee house?  Great place to introduce others to the board gaming hobby.  Have no one to share your gaming time with at any particular moment?  Solo variant works perfectly. 

Roll Through the Ages is a gem.  :)

RATING: 7
Been taking part in the playtesting version since May.  Roll Through the Ages is a fairly quick civilization lite game with a neat mechanic of using specialized dice as the way to obtain resources.  I didn't mind the push your luck feel too much because there is real decision behind whether you should stop with what you got or try one more time to get what you really need to improve your position.  I would recommend to always play with trading, as it would rob you of any sort of direct interaction otherwise.  My only reservation for it currently is its replayability.  Seems that the game is first and foremost always about maximizing the number of dice you can roll each turn, then it becomes a race to the finish by being the most efficient in either building monuments or discovering new developments.  I like it, but I don't think it's nearly substantial enough that I would want to play it again and again.

RATING: 7.5
This game has replaced all other quick games that we play. We probably play this game at least once if not more every gaming session. We use it to clean the pallet. It's the sorbet of gaming. Just played an epic game of Galaxy Trucker? Play this to clean the pallet and get ready for a more serious game of rftg. Just defeated all your enemies and became the last Lord standing in the city of Waterdeep? Maybe you should get the taste of murder out by a nice monument building game of Roll before you start to lay those tiles in Carc. This game is portable, fun, and easy to teach. Current dice obsession.

RATING: 6
It's an interesting take on the engine-building dice game concept.  Simply building one's engine is not enough, because another player can jam on the points successfully with a lesser engine; however, too weak an engine and you get nowhere.  Of course, as with most dice games, the key to winning is rolling well and making some good guesses along the way.

RATING: 6
This game is infinitely better than yahtzee, despite the similarities. You roll your dice, then pick which ones you’re keeping and continue until everything is locked. There are multiple uses for each die, and your strategy needs to change depending on your opponents’ tactics. The one thing I didn’t like too much was that an early inability to roll workers will put you behind, as nearly everyone goes after extra dice first.
